    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="3858ac0e3b214f679ec7b60cb676a1cf">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '64aa648a-97d0-4df0-962d-f539d7e89165'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d15694237" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layered iron (Fe) -based fluorophosphates, Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F (NFPF) stands for a cost-effective and voltage-advantageous cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. Nevertheless, the lack of stability imposes constraints on its development and the decay mechanism remains shrouded in ambiguity. Herein, this work proposes the breakup of Fe─F bond in octahedral dimer accountable for the dissolution of redox centers and the formation of electrochemically inert phase, ultimately leading to the deterioration of electrochemical stability. To verify and address this, Boron (B) atoms situated in interstitial positions of PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra appearing trigonal BO<sub>3</sub> can be specifically targeted to enhance bond covalency and tailor electronic rearrangements at Fe─F bonds, thus stabilizing the octahedral dimer structure. This also facilitates rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics and accelerated electronic conductivity. As expected, NFPF-B exhibits an ultra-high discharge specific capacity (118.34 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C) and excellent long-term durability (capacity retention of 91.9% after 1000 cycles). The stability of the octahedra dimer is underscored by minimal volume change (2.9%) within the two-stage biphase reaction of sodium storage mechanism. This work elucidates the enduring degradation mechanism of NFPF from octahedral dimers and offer theoretical guidance for Fe-based cathode materials with prolonged stability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202404217-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202404217-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>SIBs, heralded as one of the most promising energy storage technologies, offer a plethora of new opportunities stemming from the scarcity of lithium resources and the widespread availability of sodium. Iron (Fe) -based phosphates represent a significant category of cathode material in sodium-ion batteries, as the sodium counterpart of the well-established lithium iron phosphate, contingent upon natural abundance and environmental friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0001_R_d15694229e356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Amidst the myriads of Fe-based phosphates, Fe-based fluorophosphates, Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F (NFPF), has captured the attention of researchers owing to high theoretical specific capacity, high plateau voltage from the induce effect of F and 2D sodium ion channels.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0002_R_d15694229e365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Mater. Energy. Environ. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The NFPF exhibits a unique layered structure, characterized by the ordered arrangement of Fe/P layers and Na layers. The Fe/P layer is mainly composed of interconnected FeO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub> octahedra forming Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>F<sub>3</sub> octahedra dimer connected to PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra, while the Na layer consists of Na1 and Na2 arranged in an orderly manner. As the redox center, coordination bond chemistry and structural evolution of Fe octahedra dimer are closely related to the activity and stability. Therefore, the fine structure of Fe octahedral dimers profoundly affects the electrochemical performance of the entire material. However, NFPF has long been criticized for its poor cycling stability. Upon the analysis of material composition, it has been determined that the instability of the phosphate system may be attributed to the structural configuration of the octahedral dimer. Currently, researchers have explored various strategies, mainly focusing on carbon coating and transition metal substitution at Fe sites. The former only improves the surface electronic conductivity of materials without being able to control the intrinsic electronic conductivity or their intrinsic bond structure in octahedral dimer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0003_R_d15694229e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. ACS Appl. Energy. Mater. Adv. Sci.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Transition metals can regulate the physicochemical properties of materials such as lattice parameters, bandgap, and kinetics, but simply replacing transition metal sites cannot precisely act on Fe octahedral dimer structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0004_R_d15694229e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy. Storage. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is urgent to establish structure-activity relationship between targeted Fe octahedral dimer and the electrochemical instability of NFPF. Interestingly, the introduction of B atoms strengthens the lattice framework within the sodium-based layered oxides, specifically the robust covalent bond between boron and oxygen effectively hinders collapse of the lattice structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0005_R_d15694229e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, gradient borate polyanions have been shown to bolster the stability of lithium-rich layered cathodes by fine-tuning the electronic structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0006_R_d15694229e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Energy Lett.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, electron conductivity and the location or energy of sodium storage are crucial for understanding sodium storage behavior. Regarding B doping, it significantly reduces the bandgap, while the delocalization of electron density on the oxygen atom within the B─O bond facilitates electron transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0007_R_d15694229e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Small.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This process diminishes the electrostatic force exerted on Na<sup>+</sup> and lowers the diffusion energy barrier. Additionally, the planar triangular BO<sub>3</sub> structure promotes openings in the walls of ion diffusion channels, thereby enabling ion exchange between channels and further enhancing ion diffusion pathways.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0008_R_d15694229e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> B atoms serve as a valuable tool for strengthening the lattice framework and optimizing electronic structure. Within layered structures constructed with PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra, the incorporation of B atoms marks a breakthrough in addressing the longstanding challenge of stabilizing octahedral dimer structures, an area that has received limited attention in current literature.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, the aim is to examine the structural degradation that occurs during cycling by utilizing post-cycling phase compositions and develop targeted engineering. The persistent dissolution of transition metal and the accumulation of electrochemically inert sodium fluoride (NaF) phase have been attributed to the rupture of Fe─F bonds within the octahedra dimer, allowing for their escape from the lattice structure, which triggers structural instability and ultimately capacity decay. The introduced B atoms fall into the P-O interstitial sites to form trigonal BO<sub>3</sub> structure. This structural arrangement serves to optimize both local and electron structure, effectively enhancing the covalency of the Fe─F bond and the robustness of the octahedra dimer. Intrinsic electronic conductivity and diffusion kinetics are significantly improved with the influence of B atoms. The discharge specific capacity of NFPF-B is 118.34 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C and still maintained at 86.22 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the rate is raised to 10C. It shows superior capacity retention of up to 91.9% after 1000 cycles. The intricate sodium storage mechanism of two-stage biphase reaction characterized by slight volume change of only 2.9% is unveiled shedding light on the remarkable stability of the octahedra dimer. This groundbreaking study paves the way for new horizons and perspectives in the realm of long-term stability for layered Fe-based fluorophosphates.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202404217-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202404217-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The pristine NFPF is prepared by solid-state method. Firstly, the electrochemical performance of NFPF is examined in half cells within the window of 2.0–4.0 V. The typical galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1a</a>. NFPF behave two distinct voltage plateaus of 2.90 and 3.05 V assigned to the electrochemical behavior of each half of the Na2 site.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0009_R_d15694229e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Sustainable. Chem. Eng.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The initial discharge specific capacity of NFPF is 113.03 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. As for stability in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1b</a>, during the 200 cycles at 1C, NFPF exhibits rather rapid capacity decay and the capacity retention of only 82.07%, revealing a continuous loss of active sites in the undecorated lattice. The GCD curves for the 1st and 200th cycles at 1C are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1c</a>. For NFPF, there is a significant shortening of two platforms and an increase in polarization after cycling, underlining severe active sites loss and the material structure collapse. In order to unravel the veil of rapid capacity degradation, one detailed survey of the destruction mechanism was carried out. The cycled battery was carefully disassembled and the cathode and anode are extracted for further characterization. At the sodium anode side, the Fe content detected grows rapidly with the increase in the count of cycles, labeling that Fe will be constantly dissolved during charging and discharging process in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1d</a>. The solubilization of Fe is an indicator of local structural disruption, especially for octahedra dimer. In order to further explore the intrinsic origins evolution, the NFPF cathode after cycling was examined by XRD in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1e</a>. Besides the diffraction peaks of the NFPF and the aluminum collector, the additional signal peaks of NaF located at 38.8° are captured. The NaF content is roughly defined in terms of the ratio of the intensity of the (133) crystal plane, the strongest peak of the main peak, to the peak intensity of the characteristic peak of NaF, where (133) approximately stands for the portion of NFPF. The calculated ratios of NFPF are 4.04 and 2.36 after 100 and 200 cycles. Since the number of cycles deepens gradually, the content of NaF is accumulating. The NaF phase is viewed as electrochemically inert and its continuous generation inevitably deteriorates the electrochemical performance of the material. Aiming at locating the NaF content more accurately, the XRD patterns were refined to obtain the ratio of the main phase to the NaF phase (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The NaF/NFPF molar ratio in the NFPF cathode was 22 at the 200th cycle as derived by refinement. The Fe<sup>3+</sup> content in the cathode at the discharged state is also evidenced due to the loss of Fe to increase oxidation state to maintain charge balance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Further, High-resolution transmission scanning electron microscopy (HRTEM) is employed to characterize the morphology of the cycled cathode of NFP. As for the NFPF in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1g</a>, clear multilayering was observed in one particle, delineated by the dotted line between the darker inner layer (Region 1) and the brighter outer layer (Region 2). In HRTEM image of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1h</a>, distinct lattice discrepancies distinguish the boundary between the two phases, with the wider lattice spacing d = 0.271 nm corresponding to the (024) crystal plane of NFPF and the narrower lattice spacing d = 0.231 nm identifying to the (200) crystal plane in NaF. Moreover, lattice fringes of NFPF (d = 0.446 nm) and NaF (d = 0.229 nm) can be discerned in the interior of region 1 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1i</a>) and region 2 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1j</a>), respectively. Despite the interference of binder and unwashed sodium salts, for the element mapping, the scope of elemental Fe is significantly smaller than that of elements Na and F whose distributions are comparable (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0001">1k</a>). The leakage of Fe element and the occurrence of the NaF phase are directly justified in terms of the morphological structure and elemental aspects, in accordance with the phase composition described. The above results indicate that Fe on the cathode side and NaF on the anode side are generated synchronously and the content increases with the number of cycles. In the fluorophosphate, the decay situation differs significantly from interlayer gliding observed in oxides systems. This discrepancy arises from the fact that the oxygen atoms are securely bound within stable and robust PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. Furthermore, during the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, half of the Na atoms within NFPF designated as electrochemically inert are rendered immobile, thereby ensuring the stabilization of the sodium layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0010_R_d15694229e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Following the exclusion, the focus shifts to the octahedra dimer, a structure comprised of octahedral coplanarity formed by Fe─O/F bonds. Owing to the formidable nature of P─O bonds present in the structure, any gliding within the Fe/P layer is expected to be met with greater resistance. Notably, the spatial arrangement positions the F atoms on one side of the octahedra dimer, a configuration that inevitably leads to structural instability. As a consequence, the escape of Fe from the lattice necessitates the breaking of the Fe─O/F bond where the free F will combine with Na atom to form the NaF phase, whereas O can be immobilized by PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. This proposed degradation hypothesis due to Fe─F bond breaking consists mainly of, on the one hand, the loss of the active Fe redox center and the destruction of octahedra dimer, and the formation of the electrochemically inert NaF phase on the other hand.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404217-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5e479712-d5c4-4b50-9419-f73ab1d782fb/aenm202404217-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5e479712-d5c4-4b50-9419-f73ab1d782fb/aenm202404217-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3dbdd4c2-b973-41cc-a603-738ca56e229e/aenm202404217-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5e479712-d5c4-4b50-9419-f73ab1d782fb/aenm202404217-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404217-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(a) The GCD curves of NFPF at 0.1C. (b) Cycling performance of NFPF at 1C. (c) The GCD curves of NFPF for the 1st and 200th cycles at 1C. (d) Fe content on the sodium anode upon cycling for NFPF. (e) Post-cycling XRD of the NFPF cathode. (f) NaF phase/ NFPF phase molar ratio from XRD refinement in the NFPF cathode. (g–j) HRTEM images and (k) elemental mapping of the cycled NFPF cathode.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Drawing from the preceding words, it is of paramount importance to enhance the robustness of the Fe─F bond. For this purpose, targeted modifications involving B atoms are implemented. To ascertain the configuration, an in-depth analysis of the crystal structure and electronic structure was conducted. <b>Figures</b> <a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) depict the detailed lattice information derived from X-ray diffraction (XRD) by Rietveld refinement. Both samples exhibit fairly pure phase structure free of heterogeneous phases such as NaFePO<sub>4</sub> phase and NaF phase. The atomic occupancy discovers that the position of the B atoms is completely at variance with both the P and O atoms, implying that the designed B atoms will not enter into the P and O sites. To accurately trace the B sites in the lattice, the formation energies were calculated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2b</a> for B occupying different sites concerning P site, O site, P-O tetrahedral interstitial site, Fe-O interstitial sites, and Fe-F interstitial sites, among which the foreign B atom spontaneously access to the P-O tetrahedral interstitial site according to the formation energy is less than 0 and most negative. Compared to NFPF, the bond lengths of Fe2─F2/F6 bonds in NFPF-B are decreased from 2.19311 and 2.11882 Å to 2.12041 and 2.11149 Å, suggesting that the enhancement of Fe-F interactions conducive to resisting the disintegration of Fe─F bonds (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, the compactness of the average bond length and polyhedral volume signifies the stability of the octahedra dimer and decrease in lattice parameters (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), while the expansion of the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channel aids the acceleration of ion transportation. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2c</a>, the octahedra dimer touches robustness under the influence of interstitial B atoms, where Fe and F tend to bind to the extent that structural durability is heightened. In addition, Fe─F bond strengthening is also analyzed using the crystal orbital Hamiltonian population (COHP), which is a projection of Kohn-Sham states onto atomic orbitals, and the study of mutual orbital overlap population in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The main consideration is the -iCOHP, the integral -COHP, at the Fermi energy level. The -iCOHP value of NFPF-B is 0.372, which is higher than 0.367 of NFPF, accentuating the covalency of the strengthened Fe─F bond leading to the formation of an anchorage to Fe and F to maintain the structural stability of the octahedra dimer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0011_R_d15694229e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Sci.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In an attempt to investigate the effect on the electronic structure, the density of states (DOS) is adopted to describe this in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). The region where the conduction band and valence band are separated exhibits semiconductor properties. From the partial DOS (pDOS) curves, it is evident that the hybridized orbitals comprise O 2p orbital, F 2p orbital, and Fe 3d orbital, showcasing the covalent forces between atoms in the octahedra dimer. The pronounced downward shift of the hybridization region induced by B atom leads to a significantly widened bonding orbital, thereby effectively enhancing interaction and structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0012_R_d15694229e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison with NFPF (3.146 eV), NFPF-B demonstrates a narrowed bandgap (1.495 eV), suggesting an easier transition of electrons from the valence band to the conduction band and reflecting faster electron conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0013_R_d15694229e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Energy. Lett.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The prevalence of electronic conductivity is further substantiated by the reduced bandgap derived from the UV absorption spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). During the process of charge redistribution within the differential charge density in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2f</a>, the influx of enriched electrons to the Fe atoms contributes to the heightened Fe─F bonding. A nearly electrically neutral region forms around B atoms, leading to a significant reduction in resistance to nearby Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0014_R_d15694229e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The tuning of the electronic structure not only promotes greater stability but also fosters an environment conducive to fast conductivity for Na<sup>+</sup>. The coordination behavior of B and O in as-prepared material was characterized via B K-edge soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS), as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2g</a>. As anticipated, NFPF exhibited a minimal response peak of B. Conversely, in NFPF-B, discernible peaks ranging between 194 and 196 eV were observed adhere to the identity of trigonal B.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0015_R_d15694229e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This suggests that single B atom is coordinated with three O atoms to constitute trigonal BO<sub>3</sub>. The interstitial B atoms serve to optimize the local lattice structure and electronic structure, and the same is true for the transport of sodium ions. We specify the Na2 site to Na2 site hopping pathing closest to the B atom (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) and calculate the corresponding diffusion energy barriers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0002">2i</a>). The energy barrier decreases from 0.42 to 0.35 eV, accounting for the construction of smooth Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathway with the involvement of B atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0016_R_d15694229e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is firmly concluded that the injection of interstitial B atoms improves the cohesion of Fe─F bond of the octahedra dimer to rivet the Fe and F atoms, effectively suppressing the decay mechanism caused by the dissolution of Fe and the generation of the NaF phase, and substantially upgrading the structural and cyclic stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404217-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0a692ef8-b92f-4e2a-ab92-4799a3874133/aenm202404217-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0a692ef8-b92f-4e2a-ab92-4799a3874133/aenm202404217-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f2d27439-5225-498b-813c-3be0cc64895f/aenm202404217-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0a692ef8-b92f-4e2a-ab92-4799a3874133/aenm202404217-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404217-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(a) The XRD Rietveld refinement of NFPF-B. (b) The formation energy of B atoms occupying various sites. (c) Evolutionary diagram depicting the structural transformation of octahedra dimer. (d) COHP and -iCOHP curves of Fe─F bonds in NFPF-B. (e) The pDOS and tDOS profiles for NFPF-B. (f) Differential charge diagram of NFPF and NFPF-B. (g) B K-edge sXAS of NFPF and NFPF-B. (h) Schematic diagram of Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathway in NFPF-B. (i) The migration barriers of NFPF and NFPF-B.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information) was employed to verify the chemical valence of the elements. The high-resolution Fe 2p spectra revealed two distinct signal peaks at 711.48 and 724.88 eV, indicative of the divalent valence state of Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0017_R_d15694229e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the presence of B was corroborated by the significant response observed in the high-resolution B 1s spectra.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0018_R_d15694229e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small. Methods.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Evident B 1s signals are discerned through XPS at varying etching depths, exhibiting minimal fluctuations in peak intensity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). This strongly indicates that the B atoms are not restricted to the surface but are uniformly distributed throughout the material. To elucidate the elemental composition of B, the Inductively Coupled Plasma (ICP) method was employed to ascertain the precise elemental, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). Based on the ICP findings, the chemical formula for the B-doped NFPF is determined to be Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F-0.02B. Raman spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) indicated a ratio of I<sub>D</sub> to I<sub>G</sub> at 1.077, suggesting that the partially graphitized carbon layer ensures fundamental electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0019_R_d15694229e688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, Fourier transform infrared spectra (FT-IR) display absorption signals at 519–640 cm<sup>−1</sup> attributed to stretching vibrations of the Fe─O bond while peaks located at 920–1185 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponded to vibrations of phosphates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0020_R_d15694229e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The absorption peak at 1620 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the B─O stretching vibration in the BO<sub>3</sub> structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0021_R_d15694229e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Adv. Ceram.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The carbon content was quantified using a carbon and sulfur analyzer, revealing values for NFPF and NFPF-B at only 3.74% and 4.44%, respectively. The HRTEM in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a> (Supporting Information) reveals the presence of a uniform amorphous carbon layer, measuring at a thickness of 3 nm, encapsulating the bulk material. This conductive structure effectively facilitates an accelerated transport channel for electrons, thereby enhancing the electronic conductivity of the material. Obvious lattice fringes are well resolved with a spacing of 0.298 nm, pointing out the presence of the (042) crystal plane of NFPF-B, as confirmed by the fast Fourier transform (FFT) mode analysis. Additionally, the EDS pattern of Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrates a homogeneous distribution of Na, Fe, P, O, and F elements within the material. We employed electron probe microanalysis (EPMA) for detailed compositional assessment at microzones. As illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), the elemental distribution of B completely overlaps with those of Fe and P, which further substantiates that boron has indeed been integrated into the material structure.</p>
         
         <p>To validate the benefits of Fe─F bond reinforcement in terms of stability and to underscore the degradation mechanism, the electrochemical performance of NFPF-B is evaluated under identical conditions. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information) displays the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves at the scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Two sets of cathodic and anodic peaks located at 2.8/3.0 and 3.0/3.2 V were found corresponding to Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox couples in Na+ extraction/insertion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0022_R_d15694229e738" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The narrowed electrochemical polarization is highlighted by the determination of peak positions after the introduction of B atoms originating from the reduction of the voltage gap between adjacent peaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the response value of the current was elevated, which implies the excitation of the redox activity. The initial discharge specific capacity of NFPF-B is 118.34 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> outweighing that of NFPF, which imply that the sufficient utilization of the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox couple stems from the modulation of the local crystal structure and electron distribution by tiny interstitial B atom (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3a</a>). Starting from the capacity contribution of the dual-platform allocation, the same conclusion can be drawn by comparing it to the previous study where the capacity increase comes mainly from the extension of the high-voltage platform.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0023_R_d15694229e760" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy. Storage. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, smoother capacity trend is delivered in NFPF-B at 1C with the proposed capacity retention of 94.4%, validating the targeted optimization of structural stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3b</a>). Meanwhile, from the GCD curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3c</a>, the platform shortening and increased electrochemical polarization are effectively suppressed in NFPF-B. In terms of electrochemical properties, the enhancement of Fe─F bonding in octahedral dimers induced by interstitial B atoms exerts a positive effect on the structural and cyclic stability. Correspondingly, the Fe content of NFPF-B on the anode exhibits a diminished presence in comparison to that of NFPF in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3d</a>. The significant decrease in the Fe content observed in NFPF-B suggests that the incorporation of B has the potential to mitigate the phenomenon of Fe dissolution. The reduction in Fe<sup>3+</sup> content is also detected, which supports the alleviation of transition metal dissolution (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The calculated I(133)/I(NaF) of NFPF-B after 100 and 200 cycles is 26.65 and 13.03, which are much higher than that of NFPF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3e</a>). In strong contrast, the NaF/NFPF molar ratio in NFPF-B is only 6.4 at the 200th cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). The lattice fringes at the surface adjacent to the carbon layer (d = 0.393 nm) and the bulk (d = 0.331 nm) of the particle are assumed to be the crystal plane of NFPF, specifically (121) plane (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3g</a>) and (041) plane (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3h</a>), respectively, along with the absence of another phase. The distribution of the elements Fe, P, and O is highly overlapping with the material particles, slightly scaled down from the distribution of Na and F, owing to the presence with the binder and the sodium salt (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0003">3i</a>). It is thus clear that the reinforcement of Fe─F bonds by interstitial B atoms effectively suppresses the formation of electrochemically inert NaF and impedes the deterioration of active sites, thereby enhancing the stability of the octahedral dimer and achieving sustained cyclic stability of the layered structure. This also underscores the rationality of the proposed decay mechanism and emphasizes the significance of interstitial B atoms in this context.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404217-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/27220b84-d4e4-4573-8077-f52c5de44910/aenm202404217-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/27220b84-d4e4-4573-8077-f52c5de44910/aenm202404217-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f2ff63b1-6321-47c8-a195-e84f97475fa3/aenm202404217-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/27220b84-d4e4-4573-8077-f52c5de44910/aenm202404217-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404217-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(a) The GCD curves of NFPF-B at 0.1C. (b) Cycling performance of NFPF-B at 1C. (c) The GCD curves of NFPF-B for the 1st and 200th cycles at 1C. (d) Fe content on the sodium anode upon cycling for NFPF-B. (e) Post-cycling XRD of the NFPF-B cathode. (f) NaF phase/ NFPF phase molar ratio from XRD refinement in the NFPF-B cathode. (g–h) HRTEM images and (i) elemental mapping of the cycled NFPF–B cathode.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the high-rate electrochemical performance, as the rate is increased to 10C, the platform area on the curve can still be easily located signaling excellent rate performance (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4a</a>). To bring this out, NFPF is taken as the control in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4b</a> with the discharge specific capacity of 94.12, 82.69, 78.74, 71.26, and 61.96 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5C, 1C, 2C, 5C, and 10C, respectively, while the capacity of NFPF-B is increased to 108.29, 101.45, 98.55, 93.06, and 86.22 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the corresponding rate. In strong contrast, the noticeable improvement at the aspect of rate performance marks the acceleration of ion transport and electron conduction. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4c</a>, the Ragone plot indicates that NFPF-B involves superlative energy density and power density compared to most of the reported Fe-based fluorophosphates and other types of Fe-based phosphates cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0003_R_d15694229e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. ACS Appl. Energy. Mater. Adv. Sci.">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0004_R_d15694229e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy. Storage. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0024_R_d15694229e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Mater. Small. Science. J. Alloys. Compd. Small. Electrochim. Acta. J. Mater. Chem. A.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More attractively, as the elevated current density and extended endurance time, the lifetime is deemed substantial, retaining 91.9% of initial capacity for 1000 cycles with the loss of only 0.0081% per cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4d</a>). As far as cycling stability is concerned in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4e</a>, the NFPF-B exceeds the currently reported Fe-based fluorophosphate cathodes and even outperforms other types of phosphate cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0003_R_d15694229e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. ACS Appl. Energy. Mater. Adv. Sci.">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0004_R_d15694229e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy. Storage. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0024_R_d15694229e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Mater. Small. Science. J. Alloys. Compd. Small. Electrochim. Acta. J. Mater. Chem. A.">24</a>, <a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0025_R_d15694229e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the calculation of the apparent Na+ diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+, app</sub>), the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) is generally recognized as a powerful assessment method. The concrete steps of the calculation are demonstrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information). In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4f</a>, the phase transition in the biphasic reaction corresponding to the plateau region inhibits the sodiation/desodiation kinetics, resulting in higher values in the slope region than in the plateau region. In addition, the concentration of reactions in the platform area makes for a denser distribution of data points. The D<sub>Na+, app</sub> of NFPF is situated between 1.605 × 10<sup>−12</sup> and 8.996 × 10<sup>−9</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, while the introduction of interstitial atoms boosts that to the range of 2.878 × 10<sup>−12</sup>–4.186 × 10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, explaining the improvement in diffusion kinetics due to the widening of the transportation channel derived from the contraction of the octahedra dimer. From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4g</a>, it can be observed that the corresponding D<sub>Na+, app</sub> at the plateau is elevated no matter in the charge and discharge stages, which confirms that more active Na<sup>+</sup> are mobilized in favor of the excitation of the electrochemical activity of the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> couples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0026_R_d15694229e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, the improvement of D<sub>Na+, app</sub> is particularly evident at 3.0 V to interpret the prolongation of the high-potential plateau of the discharge curve. The evolution of diffusion kinetics is also supported by in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information). The distribution of relaxation time (DRT) analysis derived from the plots is employed to address variations in impedance across different frequencies (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). To categorize, the high frequencies (τ = 10<sup>−4</sup>≈10<sup>−2</sup>) are associated with sodium diffusion in the interfacial layer. The medium frequencies (τ = 10<sup>−2</sup>≈10) correspond to the charge transfer impedance (R<sub>ct</sub>), and the low frequencies (τ = 10≈10<sup>2</sup>) can be attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion impedance (R<sub>d</sub>) in the cathode bulk.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0027_R_d15694229e930" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano. Energy. Energy. Storage. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NFPF-B exhibits a lower value than the NFPF at the interface, indicating favorable ion interfacial diffusion. With increasing voltage, the R<sub>ct</sub> continues to decrease, maintaining a consistent level in both samples, although the NFPF-B consistently remains at a significantly lower level. The range and amplitude of the peaks in NFPF at low frequencies clearly surpass those of NFPF-B, providing evidence that the introduction of B enhances Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion capability. The R<sub>ct</sub> and the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> migration is fitted by the simulation of the equivalent circuit in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4i</a>. Faster electron exchange occurs at the cathode/electrolyte interface because the R<sub>ct</sub> of NFPF-B is always lower than that of NFPF. The trend in D<sub>Na+</sub> arrangement is much identical to the GITT calculations, being by a clear decline as well as centralization at the plateau. The acceleration of ion transport is reaffirmed in NFPF-B by an overall higher diffusion coefficient. Furthermore, the CV curves with various scanning rates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information) are applied to compare the reaction kinetics at particular reaction. D<sub>Na+</sub> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0004">4e</a> is obtained from the linear fitting of the peak current to the square root of the scanning rate by the Randles-Sevcik equation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0028_R_d15694229e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy. Mater. ACS Nano.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In accordance with the overall one order of magnitude lift of all oxidation/reduction peaks, the dominance of the C2 peak at the bottom matching to discharge high-potential platforms is the most significant indicative of the facilitation for the sluggish rate-controlled step (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). All of the above results resolve the superior profit of interstitial B modification for diffusion kinetics with respect to the interaction covalency of Fe and F atoms, reflecting exceptional rate performance and unwavering stability at high rates.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404217-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8794c1fc-1a3e-4633-bd57-c545fd4c35ed/aenm202404217-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8794c1fc-1a3e-4633-bd57-c545fd4c35ed/aenm202404217-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d778762-80c2-416b-8782-4e09f7f40314/aenm202404217-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8794c1fc-1a3e-4633-bd57-c545fd4c35ed/aenm202404217-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404217-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(a) The GCD curves of NFPF-B at various rates. (b) Rate performance of NFPF and NFPF-B. (c) Ragone plots of Fe-based fluorophosphates and other types of Fe-based phosphates cathodes for SIBs. (d) Long-term cyclic stability of NFPF-B at 10C. (e) Radar plots of this work with reported Fe-based fluorophosphates and other types of phosphates as far as capacity retention is concerned. (f) The GITT curves and D<sub>Na+</sub> of NFPF-B. (g) Comparison of D<sub>Na+</sub> for NFPF and NFPF-B at different voltage. (h) DRT analysis from in situ EIS plots of NFPF-B. (i) R<sub>ct</sub> and D<sub>Na+</sub> values fitted from EIS curves.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To deepen the insight into the structural evolution and sodium storage mechanism of NFPF-B, in situ XRD patterns were scheduled in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0005">5a,b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information) for real-time monitoring during successive Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. At the initial open-circuit voltage (OCV) step, The XRD signal peak identified as (121), (023), (042), (004), (200), (133), (104) plane is attributed to Pbcn phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0004_R_d15694229e1014" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy. Storage. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Obviously, the (121) peak gradually moves to a higher angle with the advancement of voltage upward, which is caused by the continuous delocalization of Na atoms. The intensity of peaks (023), (042), and (004) gradually decreases until the termination of the first plateau, while two new tiny peaks appear at 31.06° and 35.69°. In addition, the offset, merging, and weakening of the peaks are concentrated on the main peaks (200), (133), and (104). The above phenomena mark the emergence of the intermediate phase N1.5FPF-B indexing to the P21/c space group.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0029_R_d15694229e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Comput. Chem.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The transition at the 2.9 V plateau of the charging curve is a phase wise flow of sodium-rich N2FPF-B to the intermediate phase N1.5FPF-B. Marching toward the second plateau up to 4 V, the intensity of the merged main peak and the signals of the intermediate phases gradually diminish, and new response peaks arise at 22.85°and 26.63° near (023) and (121) plane, emphasizing the advent of the sodium-poor phase N1FPF-B.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0030_R_d15694229e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The whole process is a stepwise reaction of N2FPF-B, N1.5FPF-B to N1FPF-B, which coincides with the two plateaus. To summarize, the NFPF-B sample natures a two-stage biphasic reaction. The discharge section, symmetric mirror process, returns all peaks to their initial positions reflecting the stability and reversibility of the sodiation/desodiation of NFPF-B. Detailed lattice parameters of the in situ transition were accessed by the Rietveld refinement from XRD data in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0005">5c</a>. From a series of Na removal behaviors, both a and c show the decreasing trend with different urgency, specifically from the sodium-rich phase to the intermediate phase c that is more pronounced, and from the intermediate phase to the sodium-poor phase, which is the opposite. Parameter b, on the other hand, remains expanded throughout. The variation of lattice parameters is inevitably influenced by the different phase structures and space groups, and will also be related to the layered structure. The Fe-P and bi-Na layers constructed from octahedra dimer-linked PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra are arranged in the ac plane, and the stacking of layers is reflected in the b direction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0031_R_d15694229e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Appl. Phys.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, a and c lie in the position of the desodiation while b depends on the electrostatic force between the layers. This alternation and compensation mechanism effectively alleviates the volume effect of the biphase reaction. The calculated tiny volume change is 2.9%, strongly arguing for the superior contribution of enhanced Fe─F bond covalency anchoring Fe and F atoms to octahedra dimer and cyclic stability. In addition, the redox-centered stepwise charge compensation mechanism is characterized by ex situ XPS of Fe 2p and B 1s at different voltages, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404217-fig-0005">5d,e</a>. In the OCV state, the fitted 711.28 eV signal peak in the Fe 2p spectrum coordinates to the divalent of Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0032_R_d15694229e1044" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power. Sources.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the sodium atoms are removal, the signal peak in Fe 2p is shifted to the right, implying that part of Fe<sup>2+</sup> is oxidized to Fe<sup>3+</sup>, resulting in the coexistence of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>. When the voltage was raised to 4 V, only Fe<sup>3+</sup> at 713.78 eV was observed, showing the complete oxidation of Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404217-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404217-bib-0001_R_d15694229e1061" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Up to the discharge state, the valence state of Fe was reduced from trivalent to divalent. The B 1s signal peak remained stable over the whole period. The sufficient interconversion of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> as redox centers is responsible for the remarkable electrochemical performance of NFPF-B, with the charge compensation mechanism consistent with GCD curves and sodium storage reactions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404217-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3951fe65-a1cc-45f5-972f-ec1e0423d305/aenm202404217-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3951fe65-a1cc-45f5-972f-ec1e0423d305/aenm202404217-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6cc10444-38f7-47b0-a05f-584e2ee87b5d/aenm202404217-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3951fe65-a1cc-45f5-972f-ec1e0423d305/aenm202404217-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404217-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404217" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(a) Corresponding GCD curves and (b) 2D contour plots of in situ XRD pattern. (c) Refined lattice parameters during in situ charge–discharge processing. The ex situ XPS spectrum of (d) Fe 2p and (e) B 1s at different potentials.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>On the basis of the electrochemical performance in above-discussed coin cells, the full cell is an effective route to assess the outlook for practical applications. The full cell is fabricated by assembling NFPF-B as the cathode coupled with pre-sodiated hard carbon (HC), labeled NFPF-B//HC. It is of critical importance to note that due to the capacity disparity between the NFPF-B cathode and the HC anode, the quality of the active material in the electrodes must be matched. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information), the discharge specific capacity of the full cell at 0.1C, 0.5C, 1C, 2C, 5C, and 10C is respectively 109.8, 98.8, 91.7, 88.1, 80.9, and 72 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating excellent rate performance. Regarding the cycling stability of the full cell, the capacity retention after 120 cycles at 1C is as high as 91.5%. When the rate is increased to 5C, the capacity retains 84.5% of the initial capacity after 500 cycles. The exceptional cycling stability highlights the outstanding contribution of interstitial B atoms in strengthening the Fe─F bond and stabilizing the crystal structure. The voltage of the full cell is ≈2.85 V, slightly lower than that of the half cell, which is attributed to the higher plateau potential of HC in comparison with sodium metal. In the context of 310 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> energy density, the Ragone diagram emphasizes the advantages of NFPF-B over state-of-the-art iron-based phosphates and other types of phosphates. The aforementioned full-cell data suggest that NFPF-B is one of the more promising battery material systems for industrial development.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202404217-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202404217-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, this article posits for layered NFPF, degradation mechanism encompassing unique Fe-F bond chemistry during cycling and precisely targeted regulation by interstitial B atoms. Through the analysis of elemental content, phase composition, and morphological features post-cycling, one mechanism has been deduced, which involves the breaking of Fe─F bonds in the octahedra dimer, leading to the leaching of Fe and the generation of electrochemically inert NaF phases. Consequently, the loss of active sites results in deterioration of structural stability. In line with local crystal structure and electronic structure considerations, the octahedra dimer is stabilized by the introduction of trigonal BO<sub>3</sub> in the PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedral interstitial sites attributed to anchoring Fe and F dominated by Fe─F bond strengthening. Furthermore, intrinsic electron transportation and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics are enhanced under the influence of interstitial atoms. Building upon these advantages, NFPF-B exhibits an appealing discharge specific capacity of 118.34 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C and still reaches 86.22 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10C. It is noteworthy that the exceptional cycling performance demonstrates up to 91.9% capacity retention after 1000 cycles. An in-depth investigation into the sodium storage mechanism of the two-stage biphasic reaction reveals that minimal volume changes (only 2.9%) underscore the established robust framework. This approach paves the way for the development of highly durable NFPF cathode, offering new opportunities for engineering advancements in this field.</p>
         </section>
